$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_1$
$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_1_short$
$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_2$
$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_2_short$
$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_3$
$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_3_short$
Kawasaki Ki-32
Ki-32
The Ki-32 is Kawasaki's successful response to the Imperial Japanese Army's tender for a completely indigenously designed and constructed aircraft. The aircraft is comparable to the German Hs 123 in terms of capability with similar speed, operating altitude and payload capacity.
Aichi D1A2
D1A2
The D1A is the last Japanese aircraft to be designed by a foreign company, as Aichi requested Hansa-Brandenburg to assist in the design process. Revision to the D1A produced the D1A2, improving aerodynamics and powered by a more powerful engine. Slow introduction of the succeeding Aichi D3A meant that it served well into the late 1930s, when it was considerably obsolete.
Mitsubishi Ki-51
Ki-51
The successor to the Ki-32, the Ki-51 was able to mount a stronger engine, but with largely the same airframe and continuing to use non-retractable landing gears. Despite the outdated features, the biggest redeeming feature of the Ki-51 was that it was able to take off from rough airfields in Taiwan, Korea and China when many other aircraft cannot, being the defining close support aircraft Japan fielded.
Aichi D3A
D3A
Due to the extensive problems in the first prototype, the Aichi D3A was not accepted into service until well into 1939. The revised aircraft proved better than the Nakajima D3N1, but some directional instability remained until 1940. After resolving this problem, the D3A was found to be highly manoeuvrable compared to other aircraft of similar roles, especially planes used by the Imperial Japanese Army.
Manshū Ki-98
Ki-98
The unconventionally designed Ki-98 utilises a pusher propeller system and a 2,000 horsepower Mitsubishi engine to reach a top speed of 730 km/h. Originally designed as a high-altitude interceptor, its highly responsive control system made it also suitable for ground-attack, and the 37mm nose-mounted cannon proved to be more than capable of attacking armoured targets in China and Southern Asia.
Yokosuka D4Y Suisei
D4Y Suisei
Demand and design for a new Carrier-capable dive bomber started in 1940, but a lack of available design staff significantly delayed the introduction of the D4Y Suisei. Early prototypes used a dubiously license-produced German inline engine, while later models returned to radial engines. A slim and highly streamlined fuselage allowed it to reach high speeds in horizontal flight and in dives. This, combined with excellent manoeuvrability, gave the Suisei superior performance to contemporary dive bombers, only losing out in payload capacity.
Nakajima Type 91
Type 91
Designed in 1935, the Type 91 suffered numerous initial teething issues of poor directional stability and centre of gravity. This caused a delay into service until late into 1935 and only entered into widespread service in 1936. Despite the initial problems, the Type 91 was a competent fighter and highly respected.
Nakajima A4N
A4N
The A4N incorporated a 730 hp engine as well as an all-metal design, adapting the Army's Type 91 fighter and returning to a biplane design. Command staff and pilots significantly preferred the more conventional biplane design even if it sacrificed performance, compared to Mitsubishi's 7-shi carrier fighter prototype which crashed during testing.
Nakajima Ki-27
Ki-27
Just as the Type 91 was introduced, the Imperial Japanese Army was already looking for a monoplane replacement, but with the same armament of two light machine guns. The Ki-27 proved to be able to turn just as tightly as the preceding Type 91 with a low wing loading while also being faster.
Mitsubishi A5M
A5M
After producing a disappointing prototype for the 7-shi carrier fighter tender, Mitsubishi refined the design and produced the A5M. The new plane featured thin elliptical, inverted gull wings and a fixed undercarriage, becoming one of the first monoplanes to be deployed atop carriers.
Nakajima Ki-43 Hayabusa
Ki-43 Hayabusa
First built in 1939, the Ki-43 was a disappointment to many pilots, but a progressive series of modifications to the fighter significantly improved its performance, which made it the most popular land-based fighter of the Imperial Japanese Army with its high climb rate and manoeuvrability. However, despite the modifications, little was done to improve the firepower of the Hayabusa, as it only mounted two rifle calibre machine guns.
Mitsubishi A6M Rei-sen
A6M Rei-sen
Just as the revolutionary Mitsubishi A5M started to enter service, the IJN started to look for a successor, with detailed specifications calling for a highly capable and heavily armed fighter. Nakajima pulled out of the competition after reviewing the specifications, while Mitsubishi persevered. The A6M improved on the A5M in all but low-speed manoeuvrability, impressing navy pilots with the overall outstanding capabilities.
Nakajima Ki-84 Hayate
Ki-84 Hayate
Despite the praise the Ki-43 Hayabusa received, pilots reported disappointment over the survivability, speed and armament. The Ki-84 was designed to remedy such issues, and excelled at its role. Armed with up to four 20mm cannons, it is reportedly one of the best fighters of its era one could pilot.
Mitsubishi A7M Reppū
A7M Reppū
Ambitious admirals of the Imperial Japanese Navy sent out a tender for an even more capable aircraft than the A6M Rei-sen not long after its introduction. Mitsubishi's answer to the bid is the Reppū, with a wider wingspan, more powerful engine and significantly heavier armament compared to the A6M.
Nakajima J9N Unryū
J9N Unryū
After a German defector provided pictures and preliminary design plans of the Bf 262 to Japanese intelligence officers, the IJNAF was highly interested. Nakajima, having received a copy of these documents, sought to build their own. They cooperated with Ishikawajima to build the Ne-20 turbojet engine, and designed the aircraft to be more conventional compared to the Bf 262 with straight wings and a more aerodynamic fuselage. The resulting J9N Unryū would superficially resemble the Bf 262, but was otherwise a completely indigenous design.
Kyūshū J7W Shinden-Kai
Kyūshū J7W Shinden-Kai
J7W Shinden-Kai
J7W Shinden-Kai
The J7W Shinden was designed to mount a radial engine, but the potential of mounting a turbojet engine was also kept in mind, such as the Ishikawajima Ne-20 that was still being tested. When the turbojet engines proved their worth on the J9N Unryū, the concept was realised, and the J7W1 mounted the improved Nakajima Ne-230 engines with improvements to the fighter's armament in the form of another pair of 30mm cannons.
Mitsubishi G3M1
G3M1
After Mitsubishi bargained with Admiral Yamamoto Isoroku for backing the G3M project due to its disappointing first flight, the G3M1 was produced. Revisions to the airframe were made to allow it to carry torpedoes in addition to the standard payload of bombs. After the modifications were made, the G3M1's speed and range were unrivalled by any other great power's aircraft.
Nakajima B5N
B5N
The B5N is the first single wing torpedo bomber of the Imperial Japanese Navy, proving to be substantially faster than other models in service, but also highly vulnerable to enemy fire. It was lightly armed with only a single rear facing machine gun, but the carrying capacity and speed of the aircraft remained highly competitive among contemporaries.
Mitsubishi G4M
G4M
A further development on the G3M, demanding even longer range and higher speed, the G4M proved to be a worthy successor. It was able to carry a larger payload than the G3M, as well as capable of achieving a higher altitude and speed.
Nakajima B6N Tenzan
B6N Tenzan
The B6N design was based on its predecessor B5N, improving the speed and payload capacity without significantly changing the airframe. The engine was replaced with a Mitsubishi model, which improved the Tenzan's speed, but did little to remedy the weak defensive armament and vulnerability to enemy fire.
Yokosuka P1Y Ginga
P1Y Ginga
The 15-shi design specifications demanded a bomber capable of accompanying the A6M Rei-sen fighters with the same armament of the preceding G4M. The P1Y Ginga would be highly streamlined, allowing it to achieve a speed rivalling single engine fighters during testing. This aircraft would serve mainly as a land-based naval bomber, with variants substituting as a night fighter and interceptor due to the high speed.
Aichi B7A Ryūsei
B7A Ryūsei
The B7A Ryūsei was designed to be used aboard Japan's new generation of aircraft carriers. The distinctive inverted gull wing allowed it to carry the same payload as a B6N Tenzan but at a noticeably higher speed with much more responsive control surfaces. It also broke Japan's tradition of not mounting forward facing defensive armament on attack aircraft, with two 20mm cannons placed in the wing roots.
Kawasaki Ki-38
Ki-38
The Ki-38 was a private venture by Kawasaki as a response to the development of twin-engine heavy fighters in Europe, such as the Bf 110. After the IJA was informed of the design and inspected the wooden mock up, they ordered a number to be built and flight tested. Initial testing revealed the plane to be severely underpowered, which was somewhat remedied by fitting the Nakajima Ha-25. The design would see service over the Southeastern Pacific as a long-range patrol aircraft.
Kawasaki Ki-45 Toryū
Ki-45 Toryū
To improve the performance of the Ki-38, a series of modifications were supposed to be made. However after realising that the engine would not be powerful enough to propel the aircraft after the modifications, a new plan was drawn up. The resultant aircraft would be reworked so significantly that it was essentially a new plane, and thus it was given a new designation.
Kawasaki Ki-102
Ki-102
Despite externally resembling a Ki-45 Toryū, the Ki-102 was significantly more lethal compared to its predecessor. The 37mm Ho-204, and the later 57mm Ho-401 cannon, mounted in the nose made it a formidable aircraft when it came to bomber interception, where even a short firing pass could rip the sturdiest bombers' wing off.
Mitsubishi Ki-2
Ki-2
The Ki-2 is a Japanese adaptation of a Swedish bomber, originally designed as a German Junkers mail plane. The plane served well in reconnaissance roles and conducting light bombing. Despite being obsolete when introduced, it was still used until the end of the 1930s as a trainer.
Mitsubishi G3M
G3M
The G3M was envisioned as a fast high-altitude all-purpose bomber for the Imperial Japanese Navy. However, the underpowered Hiro Type 91 engine meant that the aircraft could not reach its projected cruising speed, let alone the operating altitude. Mitsubishi would continue to modify the aircraft, but backing from within the Navy was lacking after a disappointing first flight. It necessitated intervention from Admiral Yamamoto Isoroku to make sure the aircraft entered service and was able to prove itself.
Kawasaki Ki-48 Sōkei
Ki-48 Sōkei
Experience from developing the Ki-38 and Ki-45 project proved valuable when designing the Ki-48. The plane was lighter than its fighter counterparts and had little technical issues, with satisfactory payload capacity. Dive bomber pilots were surprised by how responsive the controls were and the capability for the aircraft to perform dive bombing.
Mitsubishi Ki-67 Hiryū
Ki-67 Hiryū
Ambitious specifications for a successor to the Ki-48 Sōkei demanded the successor to be heavily armed to fend off interceptors, capable of high speeds and dive bombing as well as the ability to survive heavy battle damage. The resultant Ki-67 would have significantly better survivability compared to contemporary Japanese aircraft with self-sealing fuel tanks and many armour plates protecting the crew.
Yokosuka R2Y2 Keiun-Kai
R2Y2 Keiun-Kai
The disappointing performance estimates of the R1Y led to a new design being commissioned by the IJN, which resulted in the R2Y, mounting the Aichi Ha-70 compound engine. An additional study led to the R2Y mounting two Mitsubishi Ne-330 turbojet engines and with extra hardpoints on the revised wings. The newly added hardpoints made the Keiun a highly capable high-speed tactical bomber.
Nakajima Ki-190 Ryūjin
Ki-190 Ryūjin
A modification of the Ki-67 Hiryū, the Army sought to improve the aircraft's speed further by mounting a pair of Mitsubishi Ne-330 turbojet engines. Initial mechanical issues aside, the Ki-190 Ryūjin achieved well in excess of 900 kmph for maximum speed during testing and was quickly adopted by the IJA. It would retain many of the features of the Ki-67 Hiryū, while enjoying the high speed provided by a turbojet engine.
Mitsubishi Ki-20
Ki-20
Mitsubishi Ki-21
Ki-21
After the initial testing and competition with the Nakajima Ki-19 design, the Ki-21 was proved to be better, having better speed. However, production was slow and the type only entered widespread service in 1939, by which the aircraft was considerably outdated and obsolete.
Nakajima Ki-49 Donryū
Ki-49 Donryū
Designed to replace the Ki-21, the Donryū was heavily armed for Japanese standard, with half a dozen light machine guns and a cannon mounted in the rear, while being capable of a higher speed and longer range than bombers in other nations.
Nakajima G8N Renzan
G8N Renzan
Despite disastrous results during initial testing and the third prototype accidentally catching fire and being destroyed, the G8N Renzan was one of the most well liked bombers. Fitted with the best turbocharger produced by Hitachi, the Renzan was able to reach a higher altitude than most Japanese aircraft, while the range or speed was comparable to any escort fighters.
Nakajima G12N Shōri
G12N Shōri
Due to the promising results of the turbo jet engine programme, the IJN requested the mechanically plagued G10N Fugaku to utilise the Nakajima Ne-230 engine. This necessitated significant rehauling of the airframe and the resulting aircraft was unrecognisable from the Fugaku, essentially being a new design, thus given a new designation and christened the Shōri.
K-200
Mitsubishi Ki-46
Ki-46
A lightly armed and fast aircraft, it outperformed the fighters in service at the time of its introduction, but an even more powerful engine was fitted afterwards. It took well into 1939 for an aircraft that was widely produced to be able to catch up to a Ki-46.
Nakajima J1N1-C
J1N1-C
Originally designed as a long-ranged bomber escort, the J1N1 Gekkō was forced into the role of an reconnaissance aircraft after most aircraft firms refused to design a dedicated aircraft due to being overloaded with designing or improving other aircraft. Nakajima suggested modifying the J1N1 Gekkō, and removed most of the forward facing armament, improving the aircraft's speed by a noticeable margin.
Nakajima Ki-34
Ki-34
Yokosuka MXY7 Ohka
Ohka
The Yokosuka MXY-7 Ohka was a purpose-built, rocket powered human-guided anti-shipping attack plane, designed to be employed should the tides of war turn against Japan's favour.
Mitsubishi J8M Shūsui
J8M Shūsui
After much difficulty, the Japanese intelligence services managed to sneak out a number of design plans for a rocket-powered interceptor from Germany. The design plans were inspected by both the IJA and IJN, and the Navy went on to commission Mitsubishi Heavy Industries to reverse engineer a copy of the aircraft, which became the J8M Shūsui, with similar performances to its contemporaries.
Mitsubishi Ki-202 Shūsui-Kai
Ki-202 Shūsui-Kai
Typical to the interservice rivalry between the IJA and IJN, the two forces disagreed on the design for the interceptor, with the IJN favouring ditching a cannon for extra fuel tanks and the IJA preferring widening the aircraft instead. Thus, the IJA ordered an independent development of the Navy's J8M Shūsui, widening the wings and creating the Ki-202 Shūsui-Kai.
Mizuno Ki-156 Shinryū
ki-156 Shinryū
The possibilities of ultra-high altitude strategic bombers bombing the Japanese mainland worried the Japanese High Command, and as such, they assigned the Yokosuka Naval Air Technical Arsenal to investigate the possibilities of a high altitude interceptor. The original specifications were then modified to use the successful Mitsubishi Toko-Ro.2 rocket motor also mounted on the J8M Shūsui, with an additional demand of being able to act as a glider. This would result in the Ki-156 Shinryū, partially designed and manufactured by Mizuno, a sports equipment company that also built gliders.

ANF Les Mureaux 117 Renseignement, Bombardement, 2-place
ANF 117 RB.2
Designed by André Brunet within the ANF Les Mureaux 110 family, the ANF 117 RB.2 is a modification of the ANF 115. As a manoeuvrable aircraft, the ANF 110 line was adopted by the French army in 1932 as a reconnaissance aircraft (ANF 113 R.2) and was constantly modernised. The ANF 117 is an unarmed, single-engine, all-metal monoplane with a fixed landing gear and reinforced wing, with bracing wires and wooden propeller. On the RB.2 version, there are suspension assemblies installed, for bombs weighing up to 400kg that can be used to support ground forces.
ANF Les Mureaux 117 Armée, Mer, Bombardement, 2-place
ANF 117 AMB.2
Designed by André Brunet within the ANF Les Mureaux 110 family, the ANF 117 AMB.2 is a carrier modification of the ANF 115. As a manoeuvrable aircraft, the ANF 110 line was adopted by the French army in 1932 as a reconnaissance aircraft (ANF 113 R.2) and was constantly modernised. The ANF 117 is an unarmed, single-engine, all-metal monoplane with a fixed landing gear, and reinforced wing with bracing wires and wooden propeller. On the AMRB.2 version there are suspension assemblies installed, for bombs weighing up to 400kg that can be used to support ground forces.
Roussel R.30 Chasse, Armée, Protection, 1-place
R.30 CAP.1
Designed by Jacques Roussel, the R.30 is a very light and fast aircraft based on the MB.150 concept, but with a decrease in the dimensions of the aircraft and the installation of a less powerful and lighter engine. Composition CAP.1, which is the main version of the French forces, is armed with two 20mm cannons and 250kg worth of bombs, surpasses its Communard counterparts in speed.
Roussel R.30 Armée, Mer, Chasse, Protection, 1-place
R.30 AMCP.1
Designed by Jacques Roussel, the R.30 is a very light and fast aircraft based on the MB.150 concept, but with a decrease in the dimensions of the aircraft and the installation of a less powerful and lighter engine. Composition AMCP.1, which is the main carrier version of the French forces, is armed with two 20mm cannons and 250kg worth of bombs surpasses its Communard counterparts in speed.
Bloch MB.174 Armée, Bombardement, 3-place
MB.174 AB.3
In 1936, the French government requested a two- or three-seat multipurpose aircraft that could be used as an attack aircraft or for reconnaissance. Bloch proposed the MB.170, a twin-engined, low-winged cantilever monoplane. The MB.174 version is a modification of the MB.170 with a new engine, after trials of which the French command asked to redesign the MB.174 as an attack aircraft. The AB.2 version is armed with two 7.5 mm cannons and can carry 400 kg worth of bombs.
Bloch MB.174 Armée, Mer, Bombardement, 3-place
MB.174 AMB.3
In 1936, the French government requested a two- or three-seat multipurpose aircraft that could be used as an attack aircraft or for reconnaissance. Bloch proposed the MB.170, a twin-engined, low-winged cantilever monoplane. The MB.174 version is a modification of the MB.170 with a new engine, after trials of which the French command asked to redesign the MB.174 as an attack aircraft. The carrier AMB.3 version is armed with two 7.5 mm cannons and can carry 400 kg worth of bombs.
Dewoitine D.510 Combat, 1-place
D.510 C.1
The D.50bis fighter project originated in 1931 at the request of the French army. The designer Émile Dewoitine, striving to achieve a high flight speed, switched from the traditional biplane scheme to a monoplane with a rather thin lower wing. Accepted in service in 1933, the D.500 was modified two years later with new engine and three-blade propeller and entered in service as D.510 - the first French plane to surpass 400 km/h.
Dewoitine D.510 Armée, Mer, Combat, 1-place
D.510 AMC.1
The D.50bis fighter project originated in 1931 at the request of the French army. The designer Émile Dewoitine, striving to achieve a high flight speed, switched from the traditional biplane scheme to a monoplane with a rather thin lower wing. Accepted in service in 1933, the D.500 was modified two years later with new engine and three-blade propeller and entered in service as D.510 - the first French plane to surpass 400 km/h.
Bloch MB.150 Chasse, 1-place
MB.150 C.1
The MB.150 is low-wing all-metal construction monoplane fighter with a retractable undercarriage and a fully enclosed cockpit. It was originally developed to conform with the requirements of the 1934 French military competition, which was seeking a new fighter design. Despite the mediocre performance, due to a number of favourable circumstances, at the beginning of 1937, the MB.150 became the basic model for the overdue rearmament of fighter squads.
Bloch MB.150 Armée, Mer, Chasse, 1-place
MB.150 AMC.1
The MB.150 is low-wing all-metal construction monoplane fighter with a retractable undercarriage and a fully enclosed cockpit. It was originally developed to conform with the requirements of the 1934 French military competition, which was seeking a new fighter design. Despite the mediocre performance, due to a number of favourable circumstances, at the beginning of 1937, the MB.150 became the basic model for the overdue rearmament of fighter squads.
Dewoitine D.520 Chasse, Bombardement 1-place
D.520 CB.1
The D.520 was designed in response to a 1937 requirement from the French military for a fast, modern fighter with a good climbing speed and an armament centred on a 20 mm cannon. The project, presented in 1938, was immediately accepted, but the military asked for an increase in speed to 505 km/h. Proven in the 1939 competitions as a faster aircraft than the modified MB.150, the D.520 is considered the most modern fighter of France.
Dewoitine D.520 Armée, Mer, Chasse, 1-place
D.520 AMC.1
The D.520 was designed in response to a 1937 requirement from the French military for a fast, modern fighter with a good climbing speed and an armament centred on a 20 mm cannon. The project, presented in 1938, was immediately accepted, but the military asked for an increase in speed to 505 km/ h. Proven in the 1939 competitions as a faster aircraft than the modified MB.150, the D.520 is considered the most modern fighter of France.
Arsenal VG-60 Chasse, Bombardement 1-place
VG.60 CB.1
The design of the aircraft with the designation VG-30 began at the end of 1937, and took place on an initiative basis without any government order. Named for engineer Michel Vernisse (V) and designer Jean Galtier (G), the VG-30 changed its design several times, until it became the VG-60 with a new 1000HP engine. The VG-60 is an all-wooden low-wing monoplane, using plywood over stringers in a semi-monocoque construction with conventional layout, and was accepted in service in the end of 1942.
Arsenal-Delanne AD.15 Armée, Mer, Chasse, 2-place
AD.15 AMC.2
The Arsenal-Delanne 15 AMC.2 is two-seat tandem wing biplane fighter, designed by Maurice Delanne and built by the Arsenal de l'Aéronautique by the request of the French Navy. Pilot and gunner seat in tandem under a single canopy at the rear of the fuselage, which gives the gunner a clear field of fire for his armament of two 7.5 mm machine guns.
Arsenal-Delanne AD.1002 Chasse, Bombardement 1-place
AD.1002 CB.1
In 1944, French aircraft manufacturers were invited by government to submit aircraft for evolution of the 'Light Weight Strike Fighter' programme. The AD.1002 was designed by Maurice Delanne and engineers of Arsenal de l'Aéronautique to meet the requirement of the French military, to a missile-carrying small mid-wing monoplane with swept wings and retractable tricycle undercarriage. Accepted in service in 1945 after initial tests, the AD.1002 is first French serial jet fighter.
Dewoitine D.6025 Combat, 1-place
Dewoitine D.6025 Combat, 1-place
D.6025 C.1
D.6025 C.1
The D.6025 is a single-seat cantilever mid-wing monoplane interceptor powered by a 5000 lb thrust engine. Armed with six 20mm cannons or four 30mm cannons, the D.6025 is third representative of the D.6020 line, modified with liquid-fuel rocket engine beneath the fuselage. Accepted in service in 1949, the D.6025 is intended to replace the AD.1002 as the main French fighter.
Latécoère Late 290 Torpille, Renseignement, 3-place
Late 290 TR.3
Designed by Latécoère in response to demands for such an aircraft, the Late 290 is based on successful Laté 283 mail plane. It is a conventional high-wing, strut-braced monoplane that carries a single torpedo externally under the fuselage. The Late 290 entered service in 1934, and by the layout is similar to its civilian predecessors.
Latécoère Late 29.0 Armée, Mer, Torpille, 3-place
Late 290 AMT.3
Designed by Latécoère in response to demands for such an aircraft, the Late 290 is based on successful Laté 283 mail plane. It is a conventional high-wing, strut-braced monoplane that carries a single torpedo externally under the fuselage. The Late 290 entered service in 1934, and is similar in layout to its civilian predecessors.
Latécoère Late 298 Torpille, Renseignement, 3-place
Late 298 TR.3
Developed since 1936, the Late 298 is intended to replace the not-so-successful Late 290 as the primary float torpedo bomber, and is also to be convertible for use as aircraft on carriers in accordance with military specifications. Being a cantilever mid-wing monoplane with automatic screw 'Ratier' equipped with electrical step control, the Late 29.8 can carry one torpedo weighing 670 kg, or 500 kg worth of bombs, and is armed with two machine guns.
Latécoère Late 298 Armée, Mer, Torpille, 3-place
Late 298 AMT.3
Developed since 1936, the Late 298 is intended to replace the not-so-successful Late 290 as the primary float torpedo bomber, and is also to be convertible for use as aircraft on carriers in accordance with military specifications. Being a cantilever mid-wing monoplane with automatic screw 'Ratier' equipped with electrical step control, the Late 298 can carry one torpedo weighing 670 kg, or 500 kg worth of bombs, and is armed with two machine guns.
Dewoitine HD.780 Torpille, Observation, 1-place
HD.780 TO.1
At the end of the summer of 1941, the French command issued a specification for a high-speed float aircraft, in accordance with the requirements of which the Dewoitine HD.780 and the aircraft carrier version HD.790 were developed. Dewoitine's designers used the D.520's fuselage almost unchanged, with the exception of modified wings, as the basis for the new aircraft.
Dewoitine HD.790 Armée, Mer, Torpille, 1-place
HD.790 AMT.1
At the end of the summer of 1941, the French command issued a specification for a high-speed float aircraft, in accordance with the requirements of which the Dewoitine HD.780 and the aircraft carrier version HD.790 were developed. Dewoitine's designers used the D.520's fuselage almost unchanged, with the exception of modified wings, as the basis for the new aircraft.
Bloch MB.130 Bombardement, Combat, Renseignement, 3-place
MB.130 BCR.3
The MB.130 is an all-metal, twin-engine, low-wing monoplane with retractable landing gear, and armed with three flexible machine guns. Developed in response to the 1936 request for a tactical bomber, the MB.130 can be used in the roles of bomber, scout or long-range escort aircraft, but the French military mostly uses the plane as a long range escort, due to the small number of aircraft that can fulfil this role.
Bloch MB.170 Armée, Bombardement, Renseignement, 3-place
MB.170 ABR.3
In 1938, the French government requested a two- or three-seat multipurpose aircraft that could be used as an attack aircraft or for reconnaissance. Bloch proposed the MB.170, a twin-engined, low-winged cantilever monoplane, which was recognised as a very promising aircraft. After initial testing, the ventral dome was removed and the project underwent significant changes, transforming the MB.170 into a heavy fighter, while development of the different versions of the MB.170 was requested.
Bloch MB.260 Bombardement, Combat, Renseignement, 3-place
MB.260 BCR.3
The MB.260, based on the captured Communard designs, is considered a reliable next-generation long-range fighter. The MB.260, developed by Société des Avions Marcel Bloch, is armed with 3 7.5mm cannons and can carry up to 400kg worth of bombs, making it a good multirole fighter.
Amiot 143 Bombardement, Combat, Renseignement, 5-place
Amiot 143 BCR.5
In 1929, the French government issued a specification for a four-seat a multi-seat combat aircraft to act as a light bomber, reconnaissance aircraft and long-range escort fighter, on which Amiot suggested its Amiot 140 design. The Amiot 140 is a high-winged cantilever monoplane of all-metal construction, with corrugated wing skinning and a fixed tail wheel undercarriage. Adopted in 1934, the modified Amiot 143 was practically retrained into a night bomber, but due to last-minute modifications, it was adopted according to the BCR classification.
Bloch MB.210 Bombardement, Combat, Nuit, 5-place
MB.210 BCN.5
The Bloch MB.210 is development of the MB.200 which was designed in response to a 1934 requirement for a new day/night bomber. The MB.210 is a twin-engined low-winged monoplane with a fixed undercarriage. Although the aircraft has established itself as an extremely durable and reliable aircraft, practically not suffering from any serious defects, it is noted by many that its thrust-to-weight ratio is insufficient, and its engines are unreliable by modern standards.
Bloch MB.230 Bombardement, Combat, 4-place
MB.230 BC.4
The MB.230 tactical bomber is built from the cell of the Bloch MB.220 transport plane. All of the passenger and cargo area has been re-designed to accommodate a bomb bay to allow the storage and the delivery of explosive ordnance. This makes the MB.230 an efficient tactical bomber, capable to strikes on both land and naval targets.
Amiot 354 Renseignement, Bombardement, 4-place
Amiot 354 RB.4
The Amiot 350 series originated in the same 1936 requirement as a rival to the MB.130. Derived from the Amiot 341 mail plane, the Amiot 350 was deemed unsuitable for the requirements of the French military, but development continued due to the potential of the new aircraft. Introduced into series production in 1943, the 354 model is a fast, cantilever, twin-engined monoplane.
Bloch MB.XIII Bombardement, Combat, 1-place
MB.XIII BC.1
The MB.X is a single-engined lightweight aircraft designed for the 'Light Weight Strike Fighter' programme by Société des Avions Marcel Bloch. Losing the competition to the AD.1002, the MB.X was accepted to further development as a bomber aircraft and entered production after significant modifications as the MB.XIII.
Dewoitine D.4050 Bombardement, Combat, 2-place
D.4050 BC.2
The D.4050 is a jet-propelled midsize combat aircraft, intended to be employed as a bomber and attack aircraft. Designed due to the negligible characteristics of the MB.XIII, the D.4050 has several variants ranging from fighters to bombers, the latter being the main type of the D.4050 of the French Air Force.
Bernard 82 Bombardement, 3-place
Bernard 82 B.3
The Bernard 82 was developed from the long-range Bernard 80 GR (Grand Raid), which had been designed to set long-distance-flight records. The all-metal Bernard 82 is a three-seat long-range bomber with one defensive mobile mount with a 7.7-mm machine gun and a possible load of 1,000 kilograms worth of bombs. Developed in 1934, the aircraft showed good range but poor speed, leading to the use of the MB.130 as support fighters for the Bernard 82.
Bloch MB.165 Bombardement, 5-place
MB.165 B.5
The Bloch MB.165 is a four-engine, long-range bomber developed by Société des Avions Marcel Bloch in 1940, from the speedy MB.160 civil transport. The original MB.165, MB.162, was considered by the French military to be insufficiently fast compared to its Communard counterparts, leading to the development of the final version, the MB.165. The aircraft can carry 3,600 kg worth of bombs, is armed with two 7.5 mm machine guns for self-defence, and has good speed even when fully loaded.
Bloch MB.175 Bombardement, 3-place
MB.175 B.3
Excellent flight performance, excellent manoeuvrability and controllability, airframe strength, were demonstrated at the very beginning of the flight test programme by the Bloch engineer group to create a specialised bomber version of Bloch MB.174-01. The MB.175 has a redesigned bomb bay capable of carrying bombs of 100-200kg each with a full load of 800kg. Considering the smaller capacity than its predecessor, the MB.175 has better performance in other areas such as speed and agility.
Bloch MB.XXII Bombardement, Combat, 1-place
MB.XXII BC.1
The MB.XXII originally was a multi-role fighter-bomber, developed as part of the 'Light Weight Strike Fighter' programme, but was rejected due to poor performance as a fighter. Converted into a long-range bomber by request of the French military, the second model was introduced in 1949 and entered service in 1950. The MB.XXII, armed with two 30mm cannons and capable of carrying bombs weighing 1,500kg, has a range of 1,600km.
Bloch MB.131 Renseignement, Bombardement, 4-place
MB.131 RB.4
The Bloch MB.131 is a monoplane reconnaissance-bomber developed in 1936 based on the MB.130 for the French government. The original MB.130 had very ordinary performance and was modified to improve the internal bomb bay and updated equipment. Introduced into service as the MB.131, the aircraft is primarily used for reconnaissance.
Dewoitine D.720 Armée, Renseignement, 3-place
D.720 AR.3
In 1938 the French Military issued a specification for a three-seat reconnaissance/army cooperation plane. The D.720, introduced at the end of 1939, is constructed largely of wood with fabric and ply-wood veneer skinning, with a rectangular section fuselage housing the crew in two glazed cockpits, and with observer seat in the nose under extensive glazing. The defensive armament consists of 4 machine guns, and the plane is suitable both for reconnaissance missions and for training new pilots.
Bloch MB.220 Troupes, Transport, 2-place
MB.220 TT
The Bloch MB.220 is a full-metal twin-engine passenger transport airplane developed to a civil use. Recognised by the French military, the MB.220 soon became the main transport aircraft of the French army and the base for the Bloch MB.230 bombers.

$NUMBER$-ya Aviatsionnaya Divizya
$NR$-ya $NAME$
$NR$-ya $NAME$
Biplane Airframe
Biplanes are considered to be stable and manoeuvrable planes with limited speed. Out of various military biplanes produced in Russia, I-15 is the most successful and legendary. I-15 and its modifications are fast and stable, and are designed to operate together with more advanced I-16 as a wingman. While most biplanes start to be outdated in 1936 as aviation innovation is flying, they still form a decent backbone of the Russian Air Fleet.
Monoplane Airframe
Seen as the future of aircraft, monoplanes revolutionised speed standards of aviation. From the first proper monoplanes, the most noteworthy model is the I-16, which quickly became a legendary plane among pilots for its high-risk, high-reward manoeuvrability: aces would wield the I-16 as the deadliest weapon, but for an untrained pilot a single mistake could turn the plane into a flying coffin.\n\nWhile the Air Fleet concentrate their efforts on fighters, first proper close air support bombers are developed due to the Army demands - multi-role BB is the first experiment by the Russian Air Force in a plane that seeks to excel in all roles - as fighter, bomber and a scout plane.
High-Speed Airframe
As war approached, the Air Force opened a programme for high-speed aircraft. The programme cemented Skorost, Sokol and Duks as main producers of fighters, each with its own approach: Skorost tries to combine civilian quality and military standards, Duks design its planes with speed and heavy armament in mind and Sokol presents mass-produced planes.\n\nAt the same time, the army concentrated their demands on armoured close air support due to which such legendary armoured assaults planes BSh-2 and BSh-6 were born.
Full-Metal Airframe
Based on frontline experiences against various enemy plane designs, Russian aircraft developers like Skorost and Seversky concentrated their efforts on standardizing, balancing and fixing minuses of existing planes, instead of developing untested new designs. Wooden aircraft became a thing of the past, while weight and every detail was tested and lightened. Each of the new planes is supposed to be not only a better version of the previous one - but a completely different plane in practice, with almost all aspects of it being perfected.
'Reactive Fighter'
Initial Russian jet planes were pioneered by Skorost, which managed to beat Sokol in their development - mainly due to successful conversion of the I-30 fighter into a jet plane. While both Sokol and Skorost are primary producers of jet aircraft for the Russian Air Fleet, Skorost planes tend to be more reliable due to its slower process of development and borrowing ideas from other companies, while Sokol often brings unexpected breakthroughs with its revolutionary designs.
Multi-Purpose Airframe
The early 1930s Russian R-6 scout from TsAGI is a scouting plane with fighter functions, which can serve as a light bomber with its 500 kg payload. Conceptually it is a 'flying cruiser', flying equivalent of multi-purpose combat ship - large and unwieldy, and master at nothing. As more specialised planes entered production, multi-purpose scout planes started to be relegated to the transport role.
Dedicated Med. Bomber
As aircraft technologies developed rapidly, it became apparent that specialised planes are needed. The Russian Air Fleet ordered development of 2 specialised bombers in 1934: the SB - TsAGI's combat plane intended for closer ranges - and the OSOAVIA's long-range bomber DB-3. These give Russia the majority of its air support capacity. The role of medium bombers is growing increasingly important, especially in Russia with its vast operational distances.
STO Development
The Special Technical Department (STO) was organized for a short period of time by the military command to develop an ideal frontline support plane. The result of this programme became the Gamayun's '100' multi-purpose airframe with the FB-2 frontline bomber and the TIS-3 heavy fighter being offshoots of it. All STO designs received high praise from the military command and pilots, with new OSOAVIA’s DB-3F and Tsagi’s SB-2 being developed as continuation of specialised projects at the same time.
Heavy Tactical Bomber
As the war progressed, medium bombers became more and more destructive, often armed with both rockets and bomb payloads rivaling those of earlier strategic bombers. However, the experience on the field showed that all previous designs lack in some aspects. DBs are inconvenient to use, PBs need to have higher payload and improved defence, FBs need better engines and TISes suffer from a multitude of issues. All these planes have potential to excel, with diligent development.
'Reactive Bomber'
Russian 'reactive bombers' is a never-ending competition between Gamayun and TsAGI. Initially losing to Gamayun’s RFB-2, TsAGI managed to return its position as the main developer of Russian tactical bombers with their RFB-4. Both developers have their pluses and minuses, and their aircraft follows pre-jet patterns - TsAGI develop spiritual successors of FB series, while Gamayun continue to use their DB series scheme.
The Flying Cruiser
The early 1930s met quite an obsession for greater 'flying cruisers', which pinnacle is the 8-engine TK bomber with 12,000 kgs (26,500 pounds) payload, produced directly with Savinkov support in 1935. Though, the most numerous 'flying cruiser' plane in the Russian service is the TB-3 bomber - the basis of Russian strategic aviation. While those planes were outdated even by 1936, and generally quite bad, they were the first Russian heavy bombers used en masse.
Long-Range Airframe
Development of long-range bombers led to the appearance of the TB-7 bomber which is a replacement for outdated flying cruisers. It is the first truly modern strategic bomber in Russian service, with quite a big payload and high range of operation.
Improved TB Series
As strategic bombers are complex to produce, merely having high bomb payload is not enough. They need to be sufficiently armed to defend themselves against much cheaper fighters. To this end, TB-8 series were developed as an attempt to fix the main problems of the earlier TB-7 series. The plane received redesigned nose and tail together with modern engines. While being a worse strategic bomber than suggested by Sikorsky DVB, it is much simpler in production and thus received approval from the military command.
DVB Series
The late war showed that standard heavy bombers are not up to their primary task of destroying enemy production facilities due to their limited range and ease of being shot down. The DVB series became the results of this revelation: there is no attempt to indulge in accurate targeting against 'military targets.' These planes are designed to be long-range high-altitude bombers, with a small fleet being able to destroy a city in one go.
RTB Series
TsAGI became the only Russian aviation developer specialised in strategic bombers, especially after Sikorsky moved to specialise in helicopters. TsAGI's RTB series is 3-engine aircraft with good flight characteristics and payload intended for continent-wide operations, but lacking range to perform effective intercontinental bombing runs. Despite this, RTB series flight characteristics are unbeatable in its class and the plane has a great room for further modernization.

Svenska Aero J 6 Jaktfalken
J 6 Jaktfalken
An old interwar biplane fighter built by Svenska Aero first in the '20s, and then by AB Svenska Järnvägsverkstädernas Aeroplanavdelning, used in all Scandinavia. Despite its efficiency for patrol and reconnaissance missions, it has had its day in our air force and will be not suitable for the requirements of the modern air warfare.
Serversky EP-106 J 9
J 9
An American built fighter, made by the Serverski company. The US air military chose its competitor, the P-36, from the Curtiss company, but it's reliable and perfectly fills our needs for our missions above Scandinavia. After some negotiations, Seversky sold us the manufacturing rights and the plans, allowing us to produce it.
FFVS J 22
J 22
The J 22, designed by Kungliga Flygförvaltningens Flygverkstad i Stockholm, is our first locally designed monoplane fighter. Appreciated by our pilots for its maniablity, it is also easy to maintain. It should allow us to defend Scandinavia efficiently from any threats.
FFVS J 27
J 27
The air warfare has evolved during the second Weltkrieg, and because of the intensive aerial fights above the Baltic, a lot of foreign planes could not find their way back to their home bases or just crashed on our shores. After there capture (or their recovery for the unluckiest ones), our technical units studied a lot these fighters of a new kind. Because the J 21 didn't met the needed requirements to be an efficient fighter, those studies resulted into the J 27, a modern fighter inspired from German and American designs, but mostly from the British Supermarine 'Spitfire'. A powerful engine, a high maniability, a contra-rotating propeller, powerful armament... our 'Norden Super Spitfire' will be a nightmare for the countries which dare to invade us!
Kjeller-SAAB J 21R
J 21R
After years of R&D, we've finally managed to build a jet fighter. The piston engine fighter J 21, which wasn't very efficient as a fighter, that's a fact. But it's a reliable and strong platform. Repowered with a jet engine and armed with 20 mm guns, the J 21R is entirely capable of defending our skies against the air invaders of the next years. No more propellers, time is for jets now.
Kjeller-SAAB 29 Tunnan
Kjeller-SAAB 29 Tunnan
Kjeller-SAAB 29
Kjeller-SAAB 29
After the good result obtained with the J 21R, time is now to build a new jet fighter, with a new cell and not rebuilt from an old aircraft. The J 29 is the climax of our air industry: it has an advanced radar, bigger fuel tanks, air to air missiles and high speed. If we continue to build planes like the 'Tunnan', the skies of Scandinavia are guaranteed for the future.
Svenska Aero J 6HS Jaktfalken
J 6HS Jaktfalken
Serversky EP-106 J 9HS
J 9HS
FFVS J 22HS
J 22HS
Kjeller-SAAB J 25HS
J 25HS
A prototype airframe. Even though the J 25HS performs well in testing, only a few airframes had been constructed before the FFVS J 27 superseded it.
SAI KZ IIA
KZ IIA
Within months of its founding, SAI, Skandinavisk Aero Industri, founded by two found technically proficient aircraft enthusiasts, produced two different sport planes. Their second model drew interest from the Danish military, which eventually contracted for the production of a ground attack variant on the craft.
SAAB B 17
B 17
After studying the latest attacker aircraft designs of the other powers in Europe, we have successfully managed to create a local aircraft capable to conduct efficiently air-to-ground support missions. The B 17, designed by SAAB, is well powered, capable of use a lot of bombs and can perform dive bombing. Its landing gears are also modular, which allows us to install skis and floats.
Kjeller-SAAB A 21
A 21
The Saab A-21 was first a fighter named J 21, but because it shown an inadequate performance for its fighter role. Despite this, the Saab design bureau realised its cell would be an excellent platform for an attacker. Its unique push-pull configuration, its powerful engine and the choice of loadout make it a powerful CAS.
SAI KZ IIA-HS
KZ IIA-HS
SAAB B 17HS
B 17HS
Kjeller-SAAB A 21HS
A 21HS
Hansa-Brandenburg S 12
S 12
SAAB T 17S
T 17S
A naval variant of our B 17, designed by Saab. The modular landing gears has been completely replaced by floats and the tail has been improved to allow him to move better on the water. It's the perfect aircraft for naval patrols or ship attack, which can also be used as a reconnaissance seaplane for our navy.
Kjeller-SAAB T 18
T 18
Svenska Aero T/J 6HS Jaktfalken
T/J 6HS Jaktfalken
The now old biplane airframe is modified to carry air-dropped torpedoes - acting more as a proof-of-concept for future designs rather than being converted en-massed as its slow speed make it an easy target in real combat situations. This acts as the foundation for future Swedish naval bombers development.
FFVS T 22HS
T 22HS
A variant of the J 22HS modified to carry air-dropped torpedoes. Its easy-to-maintain characteristics make it suitable for shipboard deployments to act as the first line of defence should the enemy fleet be sighted.
Kjeller-SAAB SB 21HS
SB 21HS
This piston-powered airframe is the first, and the last to be specifically designed as naval bombers - as Scandinavian plane manufacturers managed to perfected their jet engine construction blueprints soon after.
SAAB J 19
J 19
Our J6 and J 9 fighters are very limited for long range operations. In case of a war, they couldn't be able to escort our bombers or intercept those of the enemy at long range and high altitude. We were forced to study a new design of plane, which resulted in the J 19. Equipped with auxiliary fuel tanks and 20 mm canons, it will be able to catch the enemy, even at a far distance.
SAAB J 23
J 23
After the success of the J 19, we had to improve its design and capabilities. The J 23 is inspired by our neighbours designs and allow our air force to be more efficient. Still no radar, but no more additional fuel tanks. It's definitively a good solution to protect our neutrality from the exterior and keep an eye above the Baltic Sea.
SAAB J 24
J 24
Heavier, bigger and stronger, the Saab J 24 is a last gen interceptor. Two powerful engines, a heavy armament, a pressurised cockpit, an advanced radar and a big fuel tank allow it to conduct air interceptions even in the worst flight conditions, wherever the enemy is. It's the best plane of its kind we've ever built.

Kruengbin Kublai Baebthi-5 Prajadhipok
BorKhor.5 Prajadhipok
The Prajadhipok, also known as Fighter Type 5, was a single-seat fighter, the second indigenous Siamese aircraft design. In 1929, Lieutenant Colonel Luang Neramit Baijayonta designed the aircraft, which was named Prajadhipok after His Majesty King Prajadhipok (Rama VII). It was the second aircraft designed and built in Siam and the first Siamese designed and built fighter, now called Fighter Type 5.

Kruengbin Kublai Baebthi-6 Chumporn
BorKhor.6 Chumporn
The Chumporn was a naval version of Fighter Type-5 'Prajadhipok'. Named after the Father of Modern Royal Siamese Navy, Admiral Prince Abhakara Kiartivongse, Prince of Chumphon, this plane sadly never left the drawing board due to funding restrictions following the American Great Depresion.

Kruengbin Tingraburd Baebthi-2 Boripatra
BorTor.2 Boripatra
The Boribatra (also spelled Boriphat and Paribatra) or Bomber Type 2 was a two-seater light bomber aircraft of the 1920s designed and built by the Royal Siamese Air Force's Aeronautical Workshops. A small number were built for the Siamese Air Force, with the type being the first Siamese-designed aircraft.

Vergeltungswaffe-1
V-1
Vergeltungswaffe-2
V-2
Aggregat-10
A-10
Aero Vodochody A.101
A.101
Without any modern ground-attack aircraft, the Dual Monarchy sought to retrofit existing models to fit the role. The A.101 is the culmination of several attempts at upgrading the A.230 biplane to fill the role. Substantially larger and better armed than its predecessors, the A.101 is capable of both strafing and bombing as effectively as any of its contemporaries.
Weiss Manfréd WM.231
WM.231
Weiss Manfréd submitted the WM.23 fighter to compete with Avia's design. While its cowling-mounted, motor-driven Gebauer machine guns impressed the Committee, the B.135 was selected instead. Weiss Manfréd responded with a slight redesign, submitting it again as a dive-bomber instead to supplement the ageing fleet of A.101 biplanes in service.
Weiss Manfréd WM.44
WM.44
Designed by László Varga, it was essentially a smaller, two-men version of the WM.31 series of heavy fighters and fast bombers. Like its predecessor, it was made out of both wood and metal to increase performance. It proved more than capable, albeit lightly-armoured, for close air support missions.
Phönix D.V
D.V
With the Weltkrieg's end, the Phönix company's planned redesign of its famous D.III biplane, with the expected designation 'D.IV', never came to pass. In 1931, however, the company would resurrect the D.IV moniker with the unveiling of an entirely new biplane featuring then cutting-edge specifications. An upgraded variant of the craft, designated D.V, was adopted by the Dual Monarchy in 1933.
Avia B.534
B.534
In 1932, Renowned Bohemian engineer František Novotný began work with the Avia company on a new all-metal biplane. This effort would culminate in the B-34, which was very nearly adopted by the Dual Monarchy over the Phönix. Not to be dissuaded, the Novotný redesigned the B-34 with a new engine and chassis, as well as a fully enclosed cockpit. Unlike its predecessor, this model, the B-534, would be adopted by the Dual Monarchy.
Avia B.135
B.135
After the success of its B-534 biplane, Avia soon began work on a cutting-edge monoplane which would ultimately culminate in the B-135. It used the Austro-Daimler V12D 860 hp engine and was equipped with a 20mm autocannon in the nose and two synchronised 8mm machine guns in the cowling, giving it great manoeuvrability and accurate fire.
Avia-Ikarus B.224
B.224
Essentially the final evolution of Novotný's design, with help from Kosta Sivčev as well as the Illyrian Ikarus company, the B.224's engine was upgraded the excellent 1000 hp Austro-Daimler V12E and the machine guns were replaced by two Gebauer 12.7mm motor-driven heavy machine guns, increasing its speed and firepower over its predecessor. Notable is the new bubble canopy for the pilot and sleek fuselage.
Avia Bf.262
Bf.262
Lagging behind in the development of jet fighters, Avia acquired a licence to produce the Bf 262 for the Fliegerkorps, replacing the German 30mm guns with locally-produced 20mm guns in their slightly-revised design.
Letov L.52
Letov L.52
L.52
L.52
When Austro-Daimler developed the U2 'Uzelac' engine, Letov produced a state-of-the-art jet fighter that would become the Monarchy's first local jet aircraft adopted into service.
Letov Š.328.v
Š.328.v
The Š.328.v was the seaplane derivative of the Š.28 series. It was armed with two forward-firing 8mm machine guns and two more in a flexible mount at the rear, and had a payload capacity of around 500kg.
Lohner-Fokker T.VIII W/C
T.VIII W/C
Lohner, a company that previously made seaplanes and flying boats for the k.u.k. Seefliegerkorps during the war, acquired the rights to produce the Fokker T.VIII for the Austro-Hungarian Navy.
Weiss Manfréd WM.442
WM.442
The WM.442 is the seaplane variant of the WM.44 made for the Seefliegerkorps, intended for Adriatic and Mediterranean anti-ship operations.
Praga E.51
E.51
Initially designed as a reconnaissance aircraft, it was quickly determined that the E.51 could be quickly adapted as a heavy fighter to counter strategic bombers.
Weiss Manfréd WM.31
WM.31
The Hungarian aviation engineer László Varga designed this 3-seat fighter around experimental gas turbine engines. However, technical difficulties arose over the engines and were replaced by two Austro-Daimler V12D instead. Notably, the WM.31 was of mixed construction.
Weiss Manfréd WM.50
WM.50
The WM.50 was a rather advanced designed for its time: The two engine push-pull configuration, twin-boom aircraft by Lázló Varga was designed to significantly improve the performance of two-engine fighters to counter high-altitude bombers.
Avia F.39
F.39
Originally developed by Fokker as a transport craft, the F.IX, the Avia company quickly saw the plane's military potential, and upon receiving a licence for the aircraft, redesigned the F.IX as a bomber, the F.39. The Dual Monarchy, lacking sufficient bombers, adopted the F.39 as a stopgap while newer models were developed.
Letov Š-50
Š-50
Intending to fill the Fliegerkorps' request for a multirole two-engine, three-man crew aircraft, Letov submitted the Š-50. However, the design did not catch interest and would never be produced, with Aero's design being eventually adopted instead.
Aero Vodochody A.300
A.300
This bomber design by Antonín Husník was, when it entered service, one of the fastest aircraft in inventory, only behind the newly-adopted B.135 fighter. Fast and with a good payload, it proved to be a versatile bomber and reconnaissance aircraft.
Weiss Manfréd WM.311
WM.311
The WM.31 was quickly adapted to perform the role of a fast, tactical bomber by weakening its offensive armament and improving its payload capacity.
Letov L.11
L.11
Based on a jet fighter design by the Bayerische Flugzeugwerke, the L.11 adapted the design into a fast and light jet bomber for multiple roles, hoping to replace the Monarchy's fleet of older prop-driven tactical bombers.
Aero L.44
L.44
With the reliability of jet engines increasing and competition ever-so fierce, Aero tackled the challenge to design a next-generation fast-bomber, culminating into the L.44 fast bomber.
Avia-Dornier Do.19
Do.19
Lacking designs for strategic bombers, Avia purchased a number of Dornier four-engine bombers to showcase their potential to the air force.
Avia-Junkers Ju.290
Ju.290
Avia acquired a number of Ju.290 to supplement the small fleet of Do.19 in service.
Weiss Manfréd WM.65
WM.65
Lázló Varga's four-engine bomber design was the first domestically-produced heavy bomber of its kind in the Empire.
Aero-Flugzeugbau Friedrichshafen FF.16
FF.16
The German Flugzeugbau Friedrichshafen FF.16 would be adopted as Austria's long-range bomber, locally produced by Aero Vodochody.

Fokker D.XXI
D.XXI
The Fokker D.XXI was a low-wing monoplane fighter aircraft. It was designed in 1935 by Fokker around requirements laid out by the Royal Netherlands East Indies Army Air Force.\nThe main armament consisted of two pairs of 7.92mm M36 FN-Browning machine guns, two housed within the wings and the other pair within the forward fuselage, requiring the latter to shoot through the propeller blades; the fuselage guns carried 500 rounds each while the wing-mounted guns had 300 rounds each.
Koolhoven F.K.58
F.K. 58
The Koolhoven F.K.58 was a single engine, interceptor-fighter aircraft designed and manufactured by N.V. Koolhoven.\nIts armament consisted of 4 7.5mm guns. It had a retractable landing gear and was equipped with a more powerful 1080hp engine, allowing the plane to reach speeds of up to 505 km/h with an operational range of 750km.
De Schelde S.21
S.21
The De Schelde S.21 was a single-seat, single-engine, pusher monoplane designed by Koninklijke de Schelde N.V.\nThe S.21 was of all-metal construction, and featured an inverted gull wing, with the aircraft's tail carried on twin booms. The compact fuselage nacelle carried the pilot, engine and the aircraft's armament.\nArmament consisted of a single Madsen 23 mm cannon on a flexible mount in the nose and four 7.9 mm FN-Browning machine guns in the side of the nacelle.

Fokker T.VI (Ontwerp 115)
T.VI (Ontwerp 115)
Between August 1934 and February 1935, work began on the Ontwerp 115.\nThe Ontwerp 115 was of a four engined low-wing design with retractable landing gear and a twin rudder. The aircraft would have had four radial engines with performance calculated with the Gnome-Rhóne 14Krfs at 900Hp, the 14Krsd at 1110Hp, and the Wright Cyclone SFR-1820-F2 at 712Hp. The propellers would have been three bladed and adjustable in-flight. Bombing capacity was projected at two tonnes.

Aero Vodochody A.101
A.101
Without any modern ground-attack aircraft, the Dual Monarchy sought to retrofit existing models to fit the role. The A.101 is the culmination of several attempts at upgrading the A.230 biplane to fill the role. Substantially larger and better armed than its predecessors, the A.101 is capable of both strafing and bombing as effectively as any of its contemporaries.
Weiss Manfréd WM.231
WM.231
Weiss Manfréd submitted the WM.23 fighter to compete with Avia's design. While its cowling-mounted, motor-driven Gebauer machine guns impressed the Committee, the B.135 was selected instead. Weiss Manfréd responded with a slight redesign, submitting it again as a dive-bomber instead to supplement the ageing fleet of A.101 biplanes in service.
Weiss Manfréd WM.44
WM.44
Designed by László Varga, it was essentially a smaller, two-men version of the WM.31 series of heavy fighters and fast bombers. Like its predecessor, it was made out of both wood and metal to increase performance. It proved more than capable, albeit lightly-armoured, for close air support missions.
Phönix D.V
D.V
With the Weltkrieg's end, the Phönix company's planned redesign of its famous D.III biplane, with the expected designation 'D.IV', never came to pass. In 1931, however, the company would resurrect the D.IV moniker with the unveiling of an entirely new biplane featuring then cutting-edge specifications. An upgraded variant of the craft, designated D.V, was adopted by the Dual Monarchy in 1933.
Avia B.534
B.534
In 1932, Renowned Bohemian engineer František Novotný began work with the Avia company on a new all-metal biplane. This effort would culminate in the B-34, which was very nearly adopted by the Dual Monarchy over the Phönix. Not to be dissuaded, the Novotný redesigned the B-34 with a new engine and chassis, as well as a fully enclosed cockpit. Unlike its predecessor, this model, the B-534, would be adopted by the Dual Monarchy.
Avia B.135
B.135
After the success of its B-534 biplane, Avia soon began work on a cutting-edge monoplane which would ultimately culminate in the B-135. It used the Austro-Daimler V12D 860 hp engine and was equipped with a 20mm autocannon in the nose and two synchronised 8mm machine guns in the cowling, giving it great manoeuvrability and accurate fire.
Avia-Ikarus B.224
B.224
Essentially the final evolution of Novotný's design, with help from Kosta Sivčev as well as the Illyrian Ikarus company, the B.224's engine was upgraded the excellent 1000 hp Austro-Daimler V12E and the machine guns were replaced by two Gebauer 12.7mm motor-driven heavy machine guns, increasing its speed and firepower over its predecessor. Notable is the new bubble canopy for the pilot and sleek fuselage.

Bf.262

WM.50

A.300

Svenska Aero J 6 Jaktfalken
J 6 Jaktfalken
An old interwar biplane fighter built by Svenska Aero first in the '20s, and then by AB Svenska Järnvägsverkstädernas Aeroplanavdelning, used in all Scandinavia. Despite its efficiency for patrol and reconnaissance missions, it has had its day in our air force and will be not suitable for the requirements of the modern air warfare.
Serversky EP-106 J 9
J 9
An American built fighter, made by the Serverski company. The US air military chose its competitor, the P-36, from the Curtiss company, but it's reliable and perfectly fills our needs for our missions above Sweden. After some negotiations, Seversky sold us the manufacturing rights and the plans, allowing us to produce it.
FFVS J 22
J 22
The J 22, designed by Kungliga Flygförvaltningens Flygverkstad i Stockholm, is our first locally designed monoplane fighter. Appreciated by our pilots for its maniablity, it is also easy to maintain. It should allow us to defend Sweden efficiently from any threats.
FFVS J 27
J 27
The air warfare has evolved during the second Weltkrieg, and because of the intensive aerial fights above the Baltic, a lot of foreign planes could not find their way back to their home bases or just crashed on our shores. After there capture (or their recovery for the unluckiest ones), our technical units studied a lot these fighters of a new kind. Because the J 21 didn't met the needed requirements to be an efficient fighter, those studies resulted into the J 27, a modern fighter inspired from German and American designs, but mostly from the British Supermarine 'Spitfire'. A powerful engine, a high maniability, a contra-rotating propeller, powerful armament... our 'Svensk Super Spitfire' will be a nightmare for the countries which dare to invade us!
SAAB J 21R
J 21R
After years of R&D, we've finally managed to build a jet fighter. The piston engine fighter J 21, which wasn't very efficient as a fighter, that's a fact. But it's a reliable and strong platform. Repowered with a jet engine and armed with 20 mm guns, the J 21R is entirely capable of defending our skies against the air invaders of the next years. No more propellers, time is for jets now.
SAAB 29 Tunnan
SAAB 29 Tunnan
SAAB 29
SAAB 29
After the good result obtained with the J 21R, time is now to build a new jet fighter, with a new cell and not rebuilt from an old aircraft. The J 29 is the climax of our air industry: it has an advanced radar, bigger fuel tanks, air to air missiles and high speed. If we continue to build planes like the 'Tunnan', the skies of Sweden are guaranteed for the future.

J 25HS

Northrop A-17 B 5
B 5
The A-17 is an American attack bomber designed and built by Northrop. It's a simple and a reliable plane, easy to maintain. It is a bit limited, but greatly plays his role of air support for our ground forces. Thanks to the American engineering, the aircraft cell isn't complex and allows us to install winter equipment, including skis for the landing gear.
SAAB B 17
B 17
After studying the latest attacker aircraft designs of the other powers in Europe, we have successfully managed to create a local aircraft capable to conduct efficiently air-to-ground support missions. The B 17, designed by SAAB, is well powered, capable of use a lot of bombs and can perform dive bombing. Its landing gears are also modular, which allows us to install skis and floats.
SAAB A 21
A 21
The Saab A-21 was first a fighter named J 21, but because it shown an inadequate performance for its fighter role. Despite this, the Saab design bureau realised its cell would be an excellent platform for an attacker. Its unique push-pull configuration, its powerful engine and the choice of loadout make it a powerful CAS.
Serversky EP-106 B 9HS
B 9HS
With the Navy finding the modified version of the B5 unsatisfied, the already existing J 9HS variant is thus modifed with extra hard point for greater bomb-carrying capacity for the this particular task instead.
SAAB B 17HS
B 17HS
SAAB A 21HS

Hansa-Brandenburg S 12

SAAB T 17S
T 17S
A naval variant of our B 17, designed by Saab. The modular landing gears has been completely replaced by floats and the tail has been improved to allow him to move better on the water. It's the perfect aircraft for naval patrols or ship attack, which can also be used as a reconnaissance seaplane for our navy.
SAAB T 18
T 18
Svenska Aero T/J 6HS Jaktfalken
T/J 6HS Jaktfalken
The now old biplane airframe is modified to carry air-dropped torpedoes - acting more as a proof-of-concept for future designs rather than being converted en-massed as its slow speed make it an easy target in real combat situations. This acts as the foundation for future Swedish naval bombers development.
FFVS T 22HS
T 22HS
A variant of the J 22HS modified to carry air-dropped torpedoes. Its easy-to-maintain characteristics make it suitable for shipboard deployments to act as the first line of defence should the enemy fleet be sighted.
SAAB SB 21HS
SB 21HS
This piston-powered airframe is the first, and the last to be specifically designed as naval bombers - as Swedish plane manufacturers managed to perfected their jet engine construction blueprints soon after.
SAAB J 19
J 19
Our J6 and J 9 fighters are very limited for long range operations. In case of a war, they couldn't be able to escort our bombers or intercept those of the enemy at long range and high altitude. We were forced to study a new design of plane, which resulted in the J 19. Equipped with auxiliary fuel tanks and 20 mm canons, it will be able to catch the enemy, even at a far distance.
SAAB J 23
J 23
After the success of the J 19, we had to improve its design and capabilities. The J 23 is inspired by our neighbours designs and allow our air force to be more efficient. Still no radar, but no more additional fuel tanks. It's definitively a good solution to protect our neutrality from the exterior and keep an eye above the Baltic Sea.
SAAB J 24
J 24
Heavier, bigger and stronger, the Saab J 24 is a last gen interceptor. Two powerful engines, a heavy armament, a pressurised cockpit, an advanced radar and a big fuel tank allow it to conduct air interceptions even in the worst flight conditions, wherever the enemy is. It's the best plane of its kind we've ever built.

$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_1$
$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_1_short$
$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_2$
$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_2_short$
$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_3$
$IMP_transport_plane_equipment_3_short$
Aero Vodochody A.101
A.101
Without any modern ground-attack aircraft, the Dual Monarchy sought to retrofit existing models to fit the role. The A.101 is the culmination of several attempts at upgrading the A.230 biplane to fill the role. Substantially larger and better armed than its predecessors, the A.101 is capable of both strafing and bombing as effectively as any of its contemporaries.
Weiss Manfréd WM.231
WM.231
Weiss Manfréd submitted the WM.23 fighter to compete with Avia's design. While its cowling-mounted, motor-driven Gebauer machine guns impressed the Committee, the B.135 was selected instead. Weiss Manfréd responded with a slight redesign, submitting it again as a dive-bomber instead to supplement the ageing fleet of A.101 biplanes in service.
Weiss Manfréd WM.44
WM.44
Designed by László Varga, it was essentially a smaller, two-men version of the WM.31 series of heavy fighters and fast bombers. Like its predecessor, it was made out of both wood and metal to increase performance. It proved more than capable, albeit lightly-armoured, for close air support missions.
Phönix D.V
D.V
With the Weltkrieg's end, the Phönix company's planned redesign of its famous D.III biplane, with the expected designation 'D.IV', never came to pass. In 1931, however, the company would resurrect the D.IV moniker with the unveiling of an entirely new biplane featuring then cutting-edge specifications. An upgraded variant of the craft, designated D.V, was adopted by the Dual Monarchy in 1933.
Avia B.534
B.534
In 1932, Renowned Bohemian engineer František Novotný began work with the Avia company on a new all-metal biplane. This effort would culminate in the B-34, which was very nearly adopted by the Dual Monarchy over the Phönix. Not to be dissuaded, the Novotný redesigned the B-34 with a new engine and chassis, as well as a fully enclosed cockpit. Unlike its predecessor, this model, the B-534, would be adopted by the Dual Monarchy.
Avia B.135
B.135
After the success of its B-534 biplane, Avia soon began work on a cutting-edge monoplane which would ultimately culminate in the B-135. It used the Austro-Daimler V12D 860 hp engine and was equipped with a 20mm autocannon in the nose and two synchronised 8mm machine guns in the cowling, giving it great manoeuvrability and accurate fire.
Avia-Ikarus B.224
B.224
Essentially the final evolution of Novotný's design, with help from Kosta Sivčev as well as the Illyrian Ikarus company, the B.224's engine was upgraded the excellent 1000 hp Austro-Daimler V12E and the machine guns were replaced by two Gebauer 12.7mm motor-driven heavy machine guns, increasing its speed and firepower over its predecessor. Notable is the new bubble canopy for the pilot and sleek fuselage.
Avia Bf.262
Bf.262
Lagging behind in the development of jet fighters, Avia acquired a licence to produce the Bf 262 for the Fliegerkorps, replacing the German 30mm guns with locally-produced 20mm guns in their slightly-revised design.
Letov L.52
Letov L.52
L.52
L.52
When Austro-Daimler developed the U2 'Uzelac' engine, Letov produced a state-of-the-art jet fighter that would become the Monarchy's first local jet aircraft adopted into service.
Letov Š.328.v
Š.328.v
The Š.328.v was the seaplane derivative of the Š.28 series. It was armed with two forward-firing 8mm machine guns and two more in a flexible mount at the rear, and had a payload capacity of around 500kg.
Lohner-Fokker T.VIII W/C
T.VIII W/C
Lohner, a company that previously made seaplanes and flying boats for the k.u.k. Seefliegerkorps during the war, acquired the rights to produce the Fokker T.VIII for the Austro-Hungarian Navy.
Weiss Manfréd WM.442
WM.442
The WM.442 is the seaplane variant of the WM.44 made for the Seefliegerkorps, intended for Adriatic and Mediterranean anti-ship operations.
Praga E.51
E.51
Initially designed as a reconnaissance aircraft, it was quickly determined that the E.51 could be quickly adapted as a heavy fighter to counter strategic bombers.
Weiss Manfréd WM.31
WM.31
The Hungarian aviation engineer László Varga designed this 3-seat fighter around experimental gas turbine engines. However, technical difficulties arose over the engines and were replaced by two Austro-Daimler V12D instead. Notably, the WM.31 was of mixed construction.
Weiss Manfréd WM.50
WM.50
The WM.50 was a rather advanced designed for its time: The two engine push-pull configuration, twin-boom aircraft by Lázló Varga was designed to significantly improve the performance of two-engine fighters to counter high-altitude bombers.
Avia F.39
F.39
Originally developed by Fokker as a transport craft, the F.IX, the Avia company quickly saw the plane's military potential, and upon receiving a licence for the aircraft, redesigned the F.IX as a bomber, the F.39. The Dual Monarchy, lacking sufficient bombers, adopted the F.39 as a stopgap while newer models were developed.
Letov Š-50
Š-50
Intending to fill the Fliegerkorps' request for a multirole two-engine, three-man crew aircraft, Letov submitted the Š-50. However, the design did not catch interest and would never be produced, with Aero's design being eventually adopted instead.
Aero Vodochody A.300
A.300
This bomber design by Antonín Husník was, when it entered service, one of the fastest aircraft in inventory, only behind the newly-adopted B.135 fighter. Fast and with a good payload, it proved to be a versatile bomber and reconnaissance aircraft.
Weiss Manfréd WM.311
WM.311
The WM.31 was quickly adapted to perform the role of a fast, tactical bomber by weakening its offensive armament and improving its payload capacity.
Letov L.11
L.11
Based on a jet fighter design by the Bayerische Flugzeugwerke, the L.11 adapted the design into a fast and light jet bomber for multiple roles, hoping to replace the Monarchy's fleet of older prop-driven tactical bombers.
Aero L.44
L.44
With the reliability of jet engines increasing and competition ever-so fierce, Aero tackled the challenge to design a next-generation fast-bomber, culminating into the L.44 fast bomber.
Avia-Dornier Do.19
Do.19
Lacking designs for strategic bombers, Avia purchased a number of Dornier four-engine bombers to showcase their potential to the air force.
Avia-Junkers Ju.290
Ju.290
Avia acquired a number of Ju.290 to supplement the small fleet of Do.19 in service.
Weiss Manfréd WM.65
WM.65
Lázló Varga's four-engine bomber design was the first domestically-produced heavy bomber of its kind in the Empire.
Aero-Flugzeugbau Friedrichshafen FF.16
FF.16
The German Flugzeugbau Friedrichshafen FF.16 would be adopted as Austria's long-range bomber, locally produced by Aero Vodochody.
Weiss Manfréd WM.21
WM.21
Praga E.55
E.55
Aero A.204
